    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A high-quality sponge gourd ( Luffa cylindrica ) genome"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-08-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Sponge gourd (Luffa cylindrica) is an important cultivated vegetable and medicinal plant in the family Cucurbitaceae. In this study, a draft genome sequence of the sponge gourd inbred line P93075 was analyzed. Using Illumina, PacBio, and 10&#215; Genomics sequencing techniques as well as new assembly techniques such as FALCON and chromatin interaction mapping (Hi-C), a chromosome-scale genome of approximately 656.19&#8201;Mb, with an N50 scaffold length of 48.76&#8201;Mb, was generated. From this assembly, 25,508 protein-coding gene loci were identified, and 63.81% of the whole-genome consisted of transposable elements, which are major contributors to the expansion of the sponge gourd genome. According to a phylogenetic analysis of conserved genes, the sponge gourd lineage diverged from the bitter gourd lineage approximately 41.6 million years ago. Additionally, many genes that respond to biotic and abiotic stresses were found to be lineage specific or expanded in the sponge gourd genome, as demonstrated by the presence of 462 NBS-LRR genes, a much greater number than are found in the genomes of other cucurbit species; these results are consistent with the high stress resistance of sponge gourd. Collectively, our study provides insights into genome evolution and serves as a valuable reference for the genetic improvement of sponge gourd."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Sponge gourd (Luffa cylindrica) is an important cultivated vegetable and medicinal plant in the family Cucurbitaceae. In this study, a draft genome sequence of the sponge gourd inbred line P93075 was analyzed. Using Illumina, PacBio, and 10&#215; Genomics sequencing techniques as well as new assembly techniques such as FALCON and chromatin interaction mapping (Hi-C), a chromosome-scale genome of approximately 656.19&#8201;Mb, with an N50 scaffold length of 48.76&#8201;Mb, was generated. Additionally, many genes that respond to biotic and abiotic stresses were found to be lineage specific or expanded in the sponge gourd genome, as demonstrated by the presence of 462 NBS-LRR genes, a much greater number than are found in the genomes of other cucurbit species; these results are consistent with the high stress resistance of sponge gourd. Collectively, our study provides insights into genome evolution and serves as a valuable reference for the genetic improvement of sponge gourd."/>

        <ul class="c-article-subject-list">

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Sponge gourd (<i>Luffa cylindrica</i>) is an important cultivated vegetable and medicinal plant in the family Cucurbitaceae. In this study, a draft genome sequence of the sponge gourd inbred line P93075 was analyzed. Using Illumina, PacBio, and 10× Genomics sequencing techniques as well as new assembly techniques such as FALCON and chromatin interaction mapping (Hi-C), a chromosome-scale genome of approximately 656.19 Mb, with an N50 scaffold length of 48.76 Mb, was generated. From this assembly, 25,508 protein-coding gene loci were identified, and 63.81% of the whole-genome consisted of transposable elements, which are major contributors to the expansion of the sponge gourd genome. According to a phylogenetic analysis of conserved genes, the sponge gourd lineage diverged from the bitter gourd lineage approximately 41.6 million years ago. Additionally, many genes that respond to biotic and abiotic stresses were found to be lineage specific or expanded in the sponge gourd genome, as demonstrated by the presence of 462 <i>NBS-LRR</i> genes, a much greater number than are found in the genomes of other cucurbit species; these results are consistent with the high stress resistance of sponge gourd. Collectively, our study provides insights into genome evolution and serves as a valuable reference for the genetic improvement of sponge gourd.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Sponge gourd [<i>Luffa cylindrica</i> (L.) Roem (<i>L. cylindrica</i>), syn. <i>L. aegyptiaca</i> Mill] is a dicotyledonous vine species belonging to the family Cucurbitaceae that originates in tropical Asia. It is an important vegetable and medicinal plant in tropical and subtropical regions globally<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Sohrab, S. S., Mandal, B., Pant, R. P. &amp; Varma, A. First report of association of tomato leaf curl virus-New Delhi with yellow mosaic disease of Luffa cylindrica in India. Plant Dis. 87, 1148–1148 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d48118e496">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Zhang, L., Shi, M., Ji, J., Hu, X. &amp; Chen, F. Gut microbiota determines the prevention effects of Luffa cylindrica (L.) Roem supplementation against obesity and associated metabolic disorders induced by high-fat diet. FASEB J. 33, 10339–10339 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d48118e499">2</a></sup>. Several recent studies have revealed that sponge gourd is a good source of carbohydrates, vitamin C, various minerals (i.e., Mg, Ca, Na, K, Fe, Cu, Zn, and Mn)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Azeez, M. A., Bello, O. S. &amp; Adedeji, A. O. Traditional and medicinal uses of Luffa cylindrica: a review. J. Med. Plants Stud.5, 102–111 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d48118e503">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Oboh, I. O. &amp; Aluyor, E. O. Luffa cylindrica—an emerging cash crop. Afr. J. Agr. Res.4, 684–688 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d48118e506">4</a></sup>, tannin, oxalate, phytin phosphorus, and phytic acid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Dairo, F. A. S., Aye, P. A. &amp; Oluwasola, T. A. Some functional properties of loofah gourd (Luffa cylindrica L., M. J. Roem) seed. J. Food, Agric. Environ.5, 97–101 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d48118e511">5</a></sup>, indicating its potential as a source of vegetable protein in human diets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Oboh, I. O. &amp; Aluyor, E. O. Luffa cylindrica—an emerging cash crop. Afr. J. Agr. Res.4, 684–688 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d48118e515">4</a></sup>. Sponge gourd has also been widely used in medicine. Alcalase or tryptic protein hydrolysates in its seeds are an effective treatment for diabetes and hypertension<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Arise, R. O. et al. In vitro Angiotesin-1-converting enzyme, α-amylase and α-glucosidase inhibitory and antioxidant activities of Luffa cylindrical (L.)M. Roem seed protein hydrolysate. Heliyon5, e01634 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d48118e519">6</a></sup>. Additionally, the leaves, seeds, and fruits of sponge gourd have been used for the treatment of various diseases, including inflammatory diseases, diarrhea, and viral infections<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Abdel-Salam, I. M., Awadein, N. E. S. &amp; Ashour, M. Cytotoxicity of Luffa cylindrica (L.) M.Roem. extract against circulating cancer stem cells in hepatocellular carcinoma. J. Ethnopharmacol.229, 89–96 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d48118e523">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Abdel-Salam, I. M., Abou-Bakr, A. A. &amp; Ashour, M. Cytotoxic effect of aqueous ethanolic extract of Luffa cylindrica leaves on cancer stem cells CD44+/24- in breast cancer patients with various molecular sub-types using tissue samples in vitro. J. Ethnopharmacol.238, 111877 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d48118e526">8</a></sup>, and the triterpenoids isolated from sponge gourd (sapogenins 1 and 2) exhibit immunomodulatory activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Khajuria, A., Gupta, A., Garai, S. &amp; Wakhloo, B. P. Immunomodulatory effects of two sapogenins 1 and 2 isolated from Luffa cylindrica in Balb/C mice. Bioorg. Med. Chem. Lett.17, 1608–1612 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d48118e530">9</a></sup>. These findings explain the high medicinal value of sponge gourd, making it a focus of recent scientific research.</p><p>The lack of reference genome sequences is a major obstacle to basic and applied biology research in <i>Luffa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Wu, H. et al. Genetic linkage map construction and QTL analysis of two interspecific reproductive isolation traits in sponge gourd. Front. Plant Sci.7, 980 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d48118e539">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wu, H. et al. Large-scale development of EST-SSR markers in sponge gourd via transcriptome sequencing. Mol. Breeding34, 1903–1915 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d48118e542">11</a></sup>. In the present study, we generated a high-quality assembly of the sponge gourd genome. The whole-genome sequence of sponge gourd was generated using the Illumina, PacBio, Hi-C, and 10× Genomics GemCode sequencing platforms, followed by de novo assembly. The final genome was 656.19 Mb (the N50 values of the contig and scaffold lengths were 8.80 and 48.76 Mb, respectively) and contained 25,508 protein-coding genes, and 63.81% of the genome was occupied by repetitive elements. The sponge gourd genome was compared with the genomes of other species in Cucurbitaceae such as <i>Benincasa hispida</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xie, D. et al. The wax gourd genomes offer insights into the genetic diversity and ancestral cucurbit karyotype. Nat. Commun.10, 5158 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d48118e548">12</a></sup><i>, Citrullus lanatus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Xu, Y. et al. The draft genome of watermelon (Citrullus lanatus) and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions. Nat. Genet.45, 51–58 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d48118e553">13</a></sup><i>, Cucumis melo</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Garcia-Mas, J. et al. The genome of melon (Cucumis melo L.). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA109, 11872–11877 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d48118e558">14</a></sup><i>, Cucumis sativus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet.41, 1275–1281 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d48118e564">15</a></sup><i>, Cucurbita moschata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Sun, H. et al. Karyotype stability and unbiased fractionation in the Paleo-Allotetraploid Cucurbita genomes. Mol. Plant10, 1293–1306 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d48118e569">16</a></sup><i>, Cucurbita pepo</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Montero-Pau, J. et al. De novo assembly of the zucchini genome reveals a whole-genome duplication associated with the origin of the Cucurbita genus. Plant Biotechnol. J.16, 1161–1171 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d48118e574">17</a></sup><i>, Lagenaria siceraria</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Peng, X. et al. Complete genome sequence of a new strain of Lagenaria siceraria endornavirus from China. Arch. Virol.163, 805–808 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d48118e579">18</a></sup>, and <i>Momordica charantia</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Urasaki, N. et al. Draft genome sequence of bitter gourd (Momordica charantia), a vegetable and medicinal plant in tropical and subtropical regions. DNA Res.24, 51–58 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d48118e585">19</a></sup> to analyze its evolution. The results presented in this study will be valuable for biosynthesis studies seeking to affirm the medicinal value of sponge gourd as well as plant breeding research for the genetic improvement of sponge gourd.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing, assembly, and quality evaluation</h3><p>To assess the genome size of the sponge gourd inbred line P93075 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig1">1</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM3">1</a>), 144.34 Gb of paired-end reads (with an insert size of 350 bp) were selected to generate 17-mer frequency data for <i>k</i>-mer analysis. The <i>k</i>-mer depth distribution was plotted against the <i>k</i>-mer frequency, with the highest peak occurring at a depth of 97 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig2">2a</a>). Based on the total number of <i>k</i>-mers (71,593,662,168 bp), the sponge gourd genome size was calculated to be ~720.33 Mb, with genome heterozygosity of 0.06% (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">1</a>). Then, the genome was assembled using PacBio single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing (92.53 Gb of raw data), 10× Genomics sequencing (87.90 Gb of raw data), and Hi-C chromosome-scale scaffolding (87.36 Gb of raw data). The assembly consisted of 328 scaffolds, with an N50 scaffold length of 18.61 Mb (total length of 656.80 Mb) and an N50 contig length of 9.44 Mb (total length of 655.84 Mb). Subsequently, the Hi-C sequencing data were aligned to the assembled scaffolds using BWA-mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li, H. &amp; Wren, J. Toward better understanding of artifacts in variant calling from high-coverage samples. Bioinformatics30, 2843–2851 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d48118e626">20</a></sup>; the complete genome was 656.19 Mb, and the N50 values of the contig and scaffold lengths were 8.80 and 48.76 Mb, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Tab1">1</a>). Finally, the scaffolds were anchored onto 13 chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig2">2b</a>), and the average length of the chromosomes was 48.9 Mb, among which the shortest was 42.2 Mb (Chr01), and the longest was 55.6 Mb (Chr04) (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" Leaf</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure"
