sponge gourd genome. <b>b</b> Hi-C contact map data analysis. <b>c</b> Circular diagram showing the genetic collinearity among sponge gourd chromosomes. The concentric circles (from inside to outside) are as follows: gene density, LTR density, and collinear genome blocks, which are connected by curved lines of the same color for each element. All data are rendered based on a 200-kb window size</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of the final assembly of the sponge gourd genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A map connecting homologous regions of the genome is shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig2">2c</a>. Overall, 99.51% of the raw reads could be mapped to the assembly, which indicates that our assembly includes almost all of the information contained in the raw reads. The analysis of core eukaryotic genes revealed homologs of 91.53% of the conserved genes in the assembly (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">3</a>). The genome was also assessed using the BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Seppey, M., Manni, M. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness. Methods Mol. Biol.1962, 227–245 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1019">21</a></sup> gene set, which includes 2121 single-copy orthologous genes, and the results indicated that 95.5% of the conserved genes were found in the sponge gourd genome (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">4</a>). Approximately 99.51% of the reads could be mapped to the assembly, which covered 99.74% of the assembled sequence (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">5</a>). In summary, all these results support the high quality of the assembled sponge gourd genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation</h3><p>Tandem repeats were detected in the genome using Tandem Repeats Finder (TRF)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res.27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1037">22</a></sup>. Ultimately, repetitive sequences accounted for 63.81% of the sponge gourd genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Tab2">2</a>). Additionally, 62.62% of the genome was occupied by transposable elements (TEs), and long terminal repeats (LTRs) constituted the most abundant category of TEs, occupying 60.69% of the genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig3">3a</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Tab2">2</a>, Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">6</a>). We also predicted 781 miRNA genes, 1592 transfer RNA genes, 4682 small nuclear RNA genes, and 302 ribosomal RNA genes in the sponge gourd genome (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">7</a>). To predict protein-coding genes, we used homology-based prediction, de novo prediction, and transcriptome-based prediction based on transcriptomics analysis results derived from samples of the roots, leaves, flowers, fruits, and stems of sponge gourd. In total, 27,154 genes were predicted from the sponge gourd genome (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">8</a>). Through a combination of ab initio prediction, homology searches, and RNA sequence-aided prediction, 25,508 protein-coding genes were predicted, 93.90% of which were functionally annotated (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">9</a>). The average transcript and CDS lengths were 4184.44 bp and 1160.18 bp, respectively. The average exon and intron lengths were 241.63 bp and 795.55 bp, respectively, with 4.8 exons per gene on average. By mapping the RNA reads onto the annotated genome, we found that the majority of the RNA reads (&gt;86%) from the five sponge gourd tissues could be mapped to annotated exon regions, which were associated with the transcription of 19,739 genes.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Summary of repeat content in the sponge gourd genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Whole-genome duplication and genetic collinearity analysis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Whole-genome duplication and genetic collinearity analysis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00350-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_350_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00350-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_350_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="726" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Distribution of transposable elements in the sponge gourd genome. <b>b</b> Whole-genome duplication (WGD) events detected in the genomes of <i>Luffa cylindrica</i> (Lcy), <i>Cucumis sativus</i> (Csa)<i>, C. melo</i> (Cme)<i>, Citrullus lanatus</i> (Cla), and <i>Cucurbita moschata</i> (Cmo), with the distribution of transversion substitutions at fourfold degenerate sites (4dTv) shown on the left and the divergence time between Lcy and other species shown on the right. <b>c</b> Schematic representation of synteny between <i>L. cylindrica</i> and <i>Cucumis sativus, C. melo, Citrullus lanatus</i>, and <i>Cucurbita moschata</i>. Each line connects a pair of orthologous genes between genomes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Whole-genome duplication analysis</h3><p>Whole-genome duplication (WGD) events have been common throughout plant evolution, playing a crucial role in the evolution and speciation of plants. To investigate WGDs in the sponge gourd lineage, we identified syntenic regions across the sponge gourd, cucumber (<i>Cucumis sativus</i>), melon (<i>Cucumis melo</i>), watermelon (<i>Citrullus lanatus</i>), and pumpkin (<i>Cucurbita moschata</i>) genomes. Based on the transversion substitutions identified in the fourfold degenerate sites (4DTv = 0.6) of collinear gene pairs, the core eudicot-common hexaploidy (ECH) event (occurring 115–130 MYA) and ancient cucurbit-common tetraploidy (CCT) event (occurring 90–102 MYA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Wang, J. et al. An overlooked paleotetraploidization in cucurbitaceae. Mol. Biol. Evol.35, 16–26 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1299">23</a></sup> could be identified (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig3">3b</a>). Similar to the cucumber, melon, and watermelon lineages, there was no lineage-specific whole-genome duplication (sWGD) observed in the sponge gourd lineage (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig3">3b</a>), but this contrasted with the results in the pumpkin lineage, which underwent a sWGD event after the CCT event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Wang, J. et al. An overlooked paleotetraploidization in cucurbitaceae. Mol. Biol. Evol.35, 16–26 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1310">23</a></sup>. Further synteny analysis provided a robust sequence framework for understanding the genome evolution of sponge gourd and was used to explore the factors underlying genome expansion (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig3">3c</a>). Chromosomes 3, 4, 7, and 11 of the sponge gourd genome showed the greatest synteny with the chromosomes of the other four cucurbitaceous plants, demonstrating that these chromosomes exhibit lower rearrangement rates. Sponge gourd, cucumber, melon, watermelon, and pumpkin exhibited highly conserved synteny, although extensive chromosomal rearrangement was found to have occurred in these species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Comparative genomics</h3><p>To investigate the evolution of the sponge gourd genome, we compared it with the genomes of 13 other sequenced species, including <i>Citrullus lanatus, Cucumis melo, C</i>. <i>sativus, Cucurbita moschata, C</i>. <i>pepo, Lagenaria siceraria</i>, <i>Benincasa hispida</i>, and <i>Momordica charantia</i>, which are cucurbitaceous species, and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana, Vitis vinifera, Solanum lycopersicum</i>, and <i>Glycine max</i>, as outgroups. Compared with <i>M</i>. <i>charantia, Cucurbita moschata</i>, and <i>C</i>. <i>pepo</i>, 2695 genes (1221 families) were unique to sponge gourd (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">10</a>). The GO classification of these unique genes showed that they were enriched in the DNA replication, metabolism of DNA, ATP, and carbohydrate derivatives, proton transport, biotic stimulus, and defense response categories (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM4">11</a>).</p><p>Further phylogenetic analysis allowed the divergence times between sponge gourd genes and their homologs in the other plants to be estimated, indicating that the sponge gourd lineage (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig4">4</a>) diverged from the bitter gourd lineage (<i>M. charantia</i>) approximately 41.6 million years ago, with subsequent divergence from other cucurbitaceous plants occurring approximately 32.5 million years ago. Expansions and contractions of orthologous gene families were also determined, revealing 186 expanded gene families and 37 contracted gene families in the sponge gourd lineage (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig4">4</a>). The GO classification of expanded gene families (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM5">12</a>) revealed enrichment for genes involved in nucleic acid metabolic and defense response processes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Gene family expansions and contractions and the estimated divergence time in sponge gourd and 12 other species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Gene family expansions and contractions and the estimated divergence time in sponge gourd and 12 other species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00350-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_350_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-00350-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_350_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="678" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Blue numerical value beside each node shows the estimated divergence time (MYA, million years ago). The numbers on the left and right of the slash indicate expanded and contracted gene families, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Notably, many of the lineage-specific genes and expanded genes found in sponge gourd were enriched in stress response-related GO terms (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM5">12</a>) and KEGG pathways (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM2">13</a>), including the response to stress (GO:0006950), defense response (GO:0006952), and biosynthesis of antibiotics (map01130). Toll and interleukin-1 receptor (TIR) is an N-terminal component of the nucleotide binding site (NBS) disease resistance protein family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Li, J. et al. Unique evolutionary pattern of numbers of gramineous NBS-LRR genes. Mol. Genet. Genomics283, 427–438 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1409">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang, Y. M. et al. Uncovering the dynamic evolution of nucleotide-binding site-leucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR) genes in Brassicaceae. J. Integr. Plant Biol.58, 165–177 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1412">25</a></sup>, which includes the TIR-NBS-LRR (TNL) and CC-NBS-LRR (CNL) subfamilies and is associated with ADP binding (GO:0043531) and the defense response (GO:0006952). The expanded gene families in the sponge gourd genome were significantly enriched for GO:0043531 and GO:0006952. Moreover, among the 25,508 annotated sponge gourd reference genes, 462 genes (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">14</a>) encoding enzymes and NBS-LRR domains were identified. Additionally, various copies of NBS-LRR were tandemly duplicated in sponge gourd, including FRAGSCAFF37.822(<i>Lcy05g001660</i>)/825(<i>Lcy05g001640</i>)/828(<i>Lcy05g001620</i>)/829(<i>Lcy05g001610</i>)/835(<i>Lcy05g001570</i>)/837(<i>Lcy05g001550</i>)/839(<i>Lcy05g001530</i>). Considering that no sWGD event occurred after the CCT event in the sponge gourd lineage, we inferred that the expansion of <i>NBR-LRR</i> genes has been a major cause of genome evolution through lineage-specific tandem duplications.</p><p>The fibers of sponge gourd fruit are widely used around the world. Many studies have shown that the main components of loofah fibers are cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zaafouri, K. et al. Enzymatic saccharification and liquid state fermentation of hydrothermal pretreated Tunisian Luffa cylindrica (L.) fibers for cellulosic bioethanol production. Renew. Energy114, 1209–1213 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1448">26</a></sup>. To understand the regulation of the formation of sponge gourd fibers, we analyzed genes associated with the cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin synthesis pathways based on genomic annotation. Notably, chitinase-like (CTL), caffeoyl CoA 3-<i>O</i>-methyltransferase (CCoAMT), cinnamoyl CoA reductase (CCR), caffeoyl shikimate esterase (CSE), ferulic acid/coniferaldehyde 5-hydroxylase (F5H), and laccase (LAC) genes in sponge gourd were significantly expanded compared with those in other cucurbitaceous crops, except for pumpkin, which underwent a sWGD event after the inferred CCT event (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">15</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>In this study, we produced a high-quality genome sequence for sponge gourd. By combining 10× Genomics, PacBio, and Hi-C sequencing data, we were able to assemble a genome sequence of 656.19 Mb, which covers ~91.2% of the sponge gourd genome, and these sequences were anchored to 13 protochromosomes. The N50 values of contig and scaffold lengths were 8.80 and 48.76 Mb, respectively, and the genome included 95.5% of the conserved BUSCO core gene set. Thus, this assembly is an almost complete representation of sponge gourd genome and provides a valuable reference for the study of important agronomic characteristics of sponge gourd and related species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">The evolution and genome characteristics of sponge gourd</h3><p>The sponge gourd genome (656.19 Mb) is substantially larger than that of most other sequenced cucurbitaceous species (269–469 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xu, Y. et al. The draft genome of watermelon (Citrullus lanatus) and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions. Nat. Genet.45, 51–58 (2013)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1474">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Garcia-Mas, J. et al. The genome of melon (Cucumis melo L.). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA109, 11872–11877 (2012)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1474_1">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet.41, 1275–1281 (2009)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1474_2">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sun, H. et al. Karyotype stability and unbiased fractionation in the Paleo-Allotetraploid Cucurbita genomes. Mol. Plant10, 1293–1306 (2017)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1474_3">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Montero-Pau, J. et al. De novo assembly of the zucchini genome reveals a whole-genome duplication associated with the origin of the Cucurbita genus. Plant Biotechnol. J.16, 1161–1171 (2018)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1474_4">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Peng, X. et al. Complete genome sequence of a new strain of Lagenaria siceraria endornavirus from China. Arch. Virol.163, 805–808 (2018)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1474_5">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Urasaki, N. et al. Draft genome sequence of bitter gourd (Momordica charantia), a vegetable and medicinal plant in tropical and subtropical regions. DNA Res.24, 51–58 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1477">19</a></sup> but smaller than that of wax gourd (913 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xie, D. et al. The wax gourd genomes offer insights into the genetic diversity and ancestral cucurbit karyotype. Nat. Commun.10, 5158 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1481">12</a></sup>. Sponge gourd shows much more obvious TE expansion (62.62%) than other cucurbitaceous species such as watermelon (45.2%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Xu, Y. et al. The draft genome of watermelon (Citrullus lanatus) and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions. Nat. Genet.45, 51–58 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1485">13</a></sup>, melon (14.7%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Garcia-Mas, J. et al. The genome of melon (Cucumis melo L.). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA109, 11872–11877 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1489">14</a></sup>, cucumber (24.4%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet.41, 1275–1281 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1493">15</a></sup>, and bitter gourd (15.3%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Urasaki, N. et al. Draft genome sequence of bitter gourd (Momordica charantia), a vegetable and medicinal plant in tropical and subtropical regions. DNA Res.24, 51–58 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1498">19</a></sup> but similar TE expansion to wax gourd (66.18%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xie, D. et al. The wax gourd genomes offer insights into the genetic diversity and ancestral cucurbit karyotype. Nat. Commun.10, 5158 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1502">12</a></sup>. Accordingly, this expansion may have played a crucial role in the increase in the genome size of sponge gourd.</p><p>Synteny analysis between the sponge gourd genome and those of the other four cucurbitaceous species also showed differences in the rearrangement frequency among these chromosomes. There was high collinearity between Chr 3 of the sponge gourd genome and Chr 2 of melon, Chr 3 of watermelon, Chr 10 and Chr 11 of pumpkin (consistent with the sWGD observed in pumpkin), and Chr 1 of cucumber (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig3">3c</a>). Interestingly, the genes located in high-collinearity regions were highly enriched in the carbohydrate metabolic process (GO:0005975, such as <i>Lcy03g002220, Lcy03g015120</i>, and <i>Lcy03g017280</i>) and ATPase activity categories (GO:0016887, such as <i>Lcy03g000500</i>, <i>Lcy03g007020</i>, and <i>Lcy03g013190</i>), showing that these basic developmental regulatory genes have been relatively well conserved in these five cucurbitaceous plants during their evolution.</p><p>Further WGD event analysis revealed ECH (115–130 MYA) and CCT (90–102 MYA) events (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig3">3b</a>), and pumpkin was also determined to have undergone a sWGD event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Sun, H. et al. Karyotype stability and unbiased fractionation in the Paleo-Allotetraploid Cucurbita genomes. Mol. Plant10, 1293–1306 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1534">16</a></sup> that did not occur in the sponge gourd genome, verifying the accuracy of our findings. Taken together, these results show that the expansion of repetitive sequences (especially LTRs) was a crucial contributor to the genome expansion of sponge gourd. This was consistent with the GO classification results for the expanded genes (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM5">12</a>), which showed high enrichment for genes associated with DNA replication and metabolism, such as DNA polymerase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Elisa, D., Heinz, E., Branislav, K., Petra, B. &amp; Karel, R. Role of STN1 and DNA polymerase α in telomere stability and genome-wide replication in Arabidopsis. PLos Genet.10, e1004682 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1541">27</a></sup> activity-related genes (<i>Lcy04g003450, Lcy04g003830</i> and <i>Lcy04g005130</i>). Notably, LTR expansion has also played a crucial role in the genome evolution of wax gourd, with insert expansion occurring approximately 7–11 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xie, D. et al. The wax gourd genomes offer insights into the genetic diversity and ancestral cucurbit karyotype. Nat. Commun.10, 5158 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1552">12</a></sup>, after the divergence (based on the phylogenetic analysis results) of sponge gourd from other cucurbitaceous plants (32.5 MYA) as well as the divergence of wax gourd from <i>Cucumis</i> (16.8 MYA) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#Fig4">4</a>). Therefore, it can reasonably be speculated that adaptive evolution in the wax gourd lineage resulted from selection on phenotypic variation associated with TE insertions occurring approximately 10 MYA. Further group evolution analysis with different cultivars collected from representative regions could be performed to identify evolutionary bottleneck events and the patterns of evolution among the different cucurbitaceous species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Disease defense-related family expansion</h3><p>Throughout evolution, plants experience the expansion and contraction of gene families, which are changes that underlie phenotypic evolution. Sponge gourd is much more resistant to stress, including biotic stress and abiotic stress, than other cucurbitaceous species and has been widely used as a rootstock to improve crop yields, overcome soil-borne diseases, and enhance flooding tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Liao, C. T. &amp; Lin, C. H. Photosynthetic responses of grafted bitter melon seedlings to flood stress. Environ. Exp. Bot.2, 167–172 (1996)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1570">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Lin, K. H. et al. Study of sponge gourd ascorbate peroxidase and winter squash superoxide dismutase under respective flooding and chilling stresses. Sci. Hortic. (Amst.)162, 333–340 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1573">29</a></sup>. Many expanded and lineage-specific gene families in sponge gourd were enriched for involvement in responses to biotic stress and/or abiotic stress, such as the defense response, the response to biotic stimuli, plant–pathogen interactions, and plant hormone signal transduction, which play important roles in defense against potential biotic and/or abiotic stresses. Furthermore, there were many more <i>NBS-LRR</i> genes identified in sponge gourd (462) than in the genomes of <i>Benincasa hispida</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Xie, D. et al. The wax gourd genomes offer insights into the genetic diversity and ancestral cucurbit karyotype. Nat. Commun.10, 5158 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1582">12</a></sup>, <i>Citrullus lanatus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Xu, Y. et al. The draft genome of watermelon (Citrullus lanatus) and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions. Nat. Genet.45, 51–58 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1588">13</a></sup>, <i>Cucumis melo</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Garcia-Mas, J. et al. The genome of melon (Cucumis melo L.). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA109, 11872–11877 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1595">14</a></sup>, <i>Cucumis sativus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet.41, 1275–1281 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1601">15</a></sup>, <i>Cucurbita maxima</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Sun, H. et al. Karyotype stability and unbiased fractionation in the Paleo-Allotetraploid Cucurbita genomes. Mol. Plant10, 1293–1306 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1607">16</a></sup>, and <i>Cucurbita moschata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Sun, H. et al. Karyotype stability and unbiased fractionation in the Paleo-Allotetraploid Cucurbita genomes. Mol. Plant10, 1293–1306 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1613">16</a></sup> (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM5">14</a>). All of these results are consistent with the high biotic and abiotic stress resistance of sponge gourd.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Preliminary analysis of genes associated with nutrition in sponge gourd</h3><p>Sponge gourd is an important cultivated vegetable that is rich in nutrients essential for human health, including vitamin C, arginine, and phosphorus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Azeez, M. A., Bello, O. S. &amp; Adedeji, A. O. Traditional and medicinal uses of Luffa cylindrica: a review. J. Med. Plants Stud.5, 102–111 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1629">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Parkash, A., Ng, T. B. &amp; Tso, W. W. Isolation and characterization of luffacylin, a ribosome inactivating peptide with anti-fungal activity from sponge gourd (Luffa cylindrica) seeds. Peptides23, 1019–1024 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1632">30</a></sup>. The Gene Ontology analysis results for specific gene families in sponge gourd were enriched for transferase activity related to the transfer of phosphorus-containing groups (GO:0016772) (Supplementary Tables S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM4">11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM5">12</a>), while the corresponding KEGG analysis showed significant enrichment of genes involved in arginine biosynthesis (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM2">13</a>). Phosphorus plays crucial roles in plant growth and development by regulating plant hormone transport<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Fink, J. R. et al. Diffusion and uptake of phosphorus, and root development of corn seedlings, in three contrasting subtropical soils under conventional tillage or no-tillage. Biol. Fertil. Soils52, 203–210 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1645">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Zhao, Y., Wang, T., Zhang, W. &amp; Li, X. SOS3 mediates lateral root development under low salt stress through regulation of auxin redistribution and maxima in Arabidopsis. N. Phytol.189, 1122–1134 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1648">32</a></sup>. Furthermore, arginine is important for the development of plants (especially root systems)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Xia, J., Yamaji, N. &amp; Jian, F. M. An appropriate concentration of arginine is required for normal root growth in rice. Plant Signal. Behav.9, e28717 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1653">33</a></sup> and is the precursor for the synthesis of endogenous hormone polyamines, thus improving tolerance to high salinity and other stress conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Gad, M. Z. Anti-aging effects of l -arginine. J. Adv. Res.1, 169–177 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1657">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Kasinathan, V. &amp; Wingler, A. Effect of reduced arginine decarboxylase activity on salt tolerance and on polyamine formation during salt stress in Arabidopsis thaliana. Physiol. Plant.121, 101–107 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1660">35</a></sup>; these roles are consistent with the enrichment of specific and expanded gene families in genes involved in the defense response (Supplementary Tables S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM4">11</a> and S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM5">12</a>). These results indicate the genomic changes that underlie the value of sponge gourd as a traditional edible vegetable.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Initial genome-level study on the medicinal value of sponge gourd</h3><p>In addition, the GO analysis of expanded gene families showed the enrichment of genes involved in the cellular aromatic compound metabolic process category (GO:0006725) (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM5">12</a>), which is consistent with the richness of sponge gourd in flavonoids (high content in leaves), triterpene alcohols, <i>trans</i>-ocimene, α/β-pinene, β-myrcene (high content in flowers), and many other chemical monomers and medicinally active compounds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Fernando, L. N. &amp; Grün, I. U. Headspace–SPME analysis of volatiles of the ridge gourd (Luffa acutangula) and bitter gourd (Momordica charantia) flowers. Flavour Fragr. J.16, 289–293 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1685">36</a></sup>. Flavonoids, including formononetin, genistein, and isoliquiritigenin, which are effective in cancer prevention or therapy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Peng, F., Meng, C. W., Zhou, Q. M., Chen, J. P. &amp; Xiong, L. Cytotoxic evaluation against breast cancer cells of isoliquiritigenin analogues from spatholobus suberectus and their synthetic derivatives. J. Nat. Prod.79, 248–251 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1689">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Wang, H., Liu, Y., Zenc, Z. &amp; He, W. Study on HPLC chromatographic fingerprint of anti-tumor active site SSCE of Caulis spatholobi. Zhongguo Zhongyao Zazhi36, 2525–2529 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1692">38</a></sup>, have been reported to be the main bioactive components<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Wang, H., Liu, Y., Zenc, Z. &amp; He, W. Study on HPLC chromatographic fingerprint of anti-tumor active site SSCE of Caulis spatholobi. Zhongguo Zhongyao Zazhi36, 2525–2529 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1696">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zhou, Z. Y. et al. Spatholobi Caulis extracts promote angiogenesis in HUVECs in vitro and in zebrafish embryos in vivo via up-regulation of VEGFRs. J. Ethnopharmacol.200, 74–83 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1699">39</a></sup>. Although genes involved in flavonoid biosynthesis have been identified in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Bowerman, P. A., Ramirez, M. V., Price, M. B., Helm, R. F. &amp; Winkel, B. S. J. Analysis of T-DNA alleles of flavonoid biosynthesis genes in Arabidopsis ecotype Columbia. BMC Res. Notes5, 485 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1706">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Saito, K. et al. The flavonoid biosynthetic pathway in Arabidopsis: structural and genetic diversity. Plant Physiol. Biochem.72, 21–34 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1709">41</a></sup> and leguminous plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Aoki, T., Akashi, T. &amp; Ayabe, S. Flavonoids of leguminous plants: structure, biological activity, and biosynthesis. J. Plant Res.113, 475–488 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1713">42</a></sup>, an overall understanding of the genes involved in flavonoid biosynthetic pathways in sponge gourd is lacking. Weighted gene coexpression network analysis (WGCNA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Langfelder, P. &amp; Horvath, S. WGCNA: an R package for weighted correlation network analysis. BMC Bioinformatics9, 559 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1717">43</a></sup> has contributed to the identification genes involved in the flavonoid synthesis pathway of sponge gourd, which will be assessed through analyses of RNA-Seq results in our next research project. Further biosynthesis studies are crucial for affirming the medicinal value of sponge gourd and promoting its commercial viability.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Expansion of genes involved in the synthesis of cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin</h3><p>The synthesis of cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin in plants is complex. CTLs are likely to play a key role in establishing interactions between cellulose microfibrils and hemicelluloses, thus affecting cellulose biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Sánchez-Rodríguez, C. et al. CHITINASE-LIKE1/POM-POM1 and its homolog CTL2 are glucan-lnteracting proteins important for cellulose biosynthesis in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell24, 589–607 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1729">44</a></sup>. Specifically, <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-phenylalanine is converted to lignin by deamination (PAL), hydroxylation (C3H, C4H, HCT), <i>O</i>-methylation (COMT, CCoAMT), CoA activation (4CL), and reduction (CCR, CAD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Vanholme, R., Demedts, B., Morreel, K., Ralph, J. &amp; Boerjan, W. Lignin biosynthesis and structure. Plant Physiol.153, 895–905 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1739">45</a></sup>. The genes involved in these processes were expanded in sponge gourd (Supplementary Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#MOESM1">15</a>), which may be responsible for the formation of fibers in sponge gourd fruits.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><p>Using Illumina, PacBio, 10× Genomics, and chromatin interaction mapping (Hi-C) data, a chromosome-scale genome of ~656.19 Mb in length was generated, with 25,508 protein-coding genes. This high-quality chromosome-scale assembly and functional annotation of the sponge gourd genome will facilitate evolutionary studies of the family Cucurbitaceae and the identification of candidate genes related to natural medicinal substances. Our research provides novel information that is relevant for comparative genome studies involving sponge gourd. Finally, the sponge gourd genome provides a solid foundation for future studies, not only in sponge gourd but also in other cucurbitaceous species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Plant materials</h3><p>P93075, an advanced inbred line of sponge gourd, was used for genome sequencing. Young leaves were collected and immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen. For RNA extraction, fresh plant tissues, including roots, leaves, flowers, fruits, and stems, were collected, and external contaminants were removed by washing the samples with ultrapure water three times.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">DNA extraction and Illumina library preparation</h3><p>Sponge gourd genomic DNA was extracted from young leaf tissue using the DNAsecure Plant Kit (TIANGEN, Beijing, China). Sequencing libraries with 350-bp inserts were constructed using a library construction kit (Illumina, San Diego, CA, USA) and then sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">PacBio library construction and sequencing</h3><p>A 20-kb-insert size DNA library was constructed. Then, PacBio libraries were sequenced on the PacBio Sequel platform (Pacific Biosciences, Menlo Park, CA, USA).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">10× Genomics library construction and sequencing</h3><p>DNA sample preparation was conducted using a GemCode Instrument from 10× Genomics (Pleasanton, CA, USA). A DNA sample of 1 ng was used for the GEM reaction procedure based on PCR. The library was finally sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Hi-C library construction and sequencing</h3><p>DNA from young leaves was fixed to generate the Hi-C library. The leaf cells were lysed, and the <i>Hind</i> III endonuclease was used to digest the fixed chromatin. Then, the sheared 350-bp fragments were ligated to adaptors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Eitan, Y. &amp; Amos, T. Probabilistic modeling of Hi-C contact maps eliminates systematic biases to characterize global chromosomal architecture. Nat. Genet.43, 1059 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1803">46</a></sup> and labeled with biotin. After PCR enrichment, the libraries were sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Estimation of genome size using <i>k</i>-mer analysis</h3><p>To estimate genome characteristics, <i>k</i>-mer frequency analysis was used<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Liu, B. et al. Estimation of genomic characteristics by analyzing k-mer frequency in de novo genome projects. &#xA;http://arxiv.org/abs/1308.2012v1&#xA;&#xA;. (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1822">47</a></sup>. The genome size of P93075 sponge gourd was calculated based on <i>k</i>-mer (<i>k</i> = 17) statistics.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Genome assembly</h3><p>The de novo assembly of the long reads from the PacBio SMRT Sequencer was performed by using FALCON (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON/">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON/</a>) and FALCON-Unzip<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1847">48</a></sup>. To obtain sufficient corrected reads, the longest segment with at least 60× depth (longest coverage of subreads) was selected for sequence error correction. Then, the error-corrected reads were assembled into genomic contigs with FALCON. After the initial assembly, FALCON-Unzip was used to produce primary contigs (p-contigs), which were then polished using Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1851">49</a></sup>. Next, we used BWA-MEM to align the 10× Genomics data to the assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li, H. &amp; Wren, J. Toward better understanding of artifacts in variant calling from high-coverage samples. Bioinformatics30, 2843–2851 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1855">20</a></sup>. The construction of scaffolds was performed using fragScaff with the barcoded sequencing reads. Finally, error correction was performed using Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zaafouri, K. et al. Enzymatic saccharification and liquid state fermentation of hydrothermal pretreated Tunisian Luffa cylindrica (L.) fibers for cellulosic bioethanol production. Renew. Energy114, 1209–1213 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1859">26</a></sup> based on the Illumina sequences. Subsequently, the Hi-C sequencing data were aligned to the scaffolds using BWA-mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li, H. &amp; Wren, J. Toward better understanding of artifacts in variant calling from high-coverage samples. Bioinformatics30, 2843–2851 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1864">20</a></sup>, and the scaffolds were clustered onto chromosomes using LACHESIS (<a href="http://shendurelab.github.io/LACHESIS/">http://shendurelab.github.io/LACHESIS/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Genome annotation</h3><p>RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Smit, A. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0. GItHub (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1890">50</a></sup> and LTR_FINDER (<a href="http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/ltr_finder/">http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/ltr_finder/</a>) were used for de novo repeat family identification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR-FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1901">51</a></sup>, and RepeatScout (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) was used to build the de novo repeat library. For the homology-based approach, RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>, version 4.0.5) and RepeatProteinMask (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) were used against the Repbase TE library and TE protein database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Prot. Bioinform.25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1927">52</a></sup>. TRF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res.27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1931">22</a></sup> was used to detect tandem repeats in the sponge gourd genome.</p><p>For transcriptome-based prediction, RNA-seq data were mapped to the sponge gourd genome using TopHat (version 2.0.8) and Cufflinks (version 2.1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: Accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol.14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1938">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Ghosh, S. &amp; Chan, C. K. Analysis of RNA-Seq data using TopHat and Cufflinks. Methods Mol. Biol.1374, 339–361 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1941">54</a></sup>. In addition, Trinity was used to assemble the RNA-seq data, and the resulting assembly was used to generate several pseudounigenes, which were mapped to the assembly. Finally, PASA (<a href="http://pasapipeline.github.io/">http://pasapipeline.github.io/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1952">55</a></sup> was used to predict the gene models.</p><p>BLASTP (E-value = 1e-05) analysis against the SwissProt and NR databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: A new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res.25, 3389–3402 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1959">56</a></sup> was used for functional annotation. InterProScan (V4.8) and HMMER (V3.1) analyses were performed against the InterPro and Pfam databases, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Finn, R. D. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res.38, D211–D222 (2010)." href="#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1963">57</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Finn, R. D. et al. HMMER web server: 2015 update. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, 30–38 (2015)." href="#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1963_1">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Finn, R. D. et al. InterPro in 2017-beyond protein family and domain annotations. Nucleic Acids Res.45, D190–D199 (2017)." href="#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1963_2">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan—an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1966">60</a></sup>. The tRNA genes were identified by using tRNAscan-SE software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. TRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res.25, 955–964 (1996)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1970">61</a></sup>. rRNA fragments were predicted by alignment to rRNA sequences based on BLASTn analysis (E-value of 1e-10). INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics25, 1335–1337 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1974">62</a></sup> and the Rfam database (release 9.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Griffiths-Jones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res.33, D121–D124 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1978">63</a></sup> were used to predict miRNAs and snRNAs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Gene collinearity analysis</h3><p>Matched genes with E-values &lt; 1e<sup>_</sup>5 were considered as candidate homologs. Next, ColinearScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Wang, X. et al. Statistical inference of chromosomal homology based on gene colinearity and applications to Arabidopsis and rice. BMC Bioinformatics7, 447 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d48118e1992">64</a></sup> was performed to identify syntenic blocks. Whole-genome duplication (WGD) analysis was performed using MCScanX software (<a href="http://chibba.pgml.uga.edu/mcscan2">http://chibba.pgml.uga.edu/mcscan2</a>) with the default parameters. Then, 4DTv distances were calculated for each gene pair in each syntenic block.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec24">Comparative genome analysis</h3><p>Comparative analysis was performed to identify orthologous gene families among the 13 plant species as described in the main text, including sponge gourd. For all-against-all protein BLAST searches, we first filtered out the proteins that were fewer than 50 amino acids in length and retained the longest protein among alternative splice variants. Then, we used BLASTP (E-value &lt; le-7) for searches of the filtered proteins, and we clustered them into orthologous groups using OrthoMCL with the inflation parameter set at 1.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: Identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res.13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d48118e2011">65</a></sup>. One protein per species in a cluster was clustered into the single-copy orthologs, which were used for MUSCLE alignment, and a phylogenetic tree was generated using the maximum likelihood method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: a multiple sequence alignment method with reduced time and space complexity. BMC Bioinformatics5, 113 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-00350-9#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d48118e2015">66</a></sup>. PAML MCMCtree (<a href="http://abacus.gene.ucl.ac.uk/software/paml.html">http://abacus.gene.ucl.ac.uk/software/paml.html</a>) was used to infer the divergence time of each species. CAFÉ (<a href="https://sourceforge.net/projects/cafehahnlab">https://sourceforge.net/projects/cafehahnlab</a>) was used to analyze gene family expansion/contraction.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The raw genome and transcriptome sequencing data are available from the NCBI under the project ID PRJNA596077.</p>
